**Archaea** ( [_ar-KEE-ə_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key")) is a [domain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_(biology) "Domain (biology)") of [organisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organism "Organism"). Traditionally, Archaea included only its [prokaryotic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prokaryote "Prokaryote") members, but has since been found to be [paraphyletic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paraphyletic "Paraphyletic"), as [eukaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryote "Eukaryote") are known to have evolved from archaea. Even though the domain Archaea [cladistically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cladistics "Cladistics") includes eukaryotes, the term "archaea" (sg.: archaeon [_ar-KEE-on_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key "Help:Pronunciation respelling key"), from the Greek "ἀρχαῖον", which means ancient) in English still generally refers specifically to prokaryotic members of Archaea. Archaea were initially [classified](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology) "Taxonomy (biology)") as [bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria"), receiving the name archaebacteria (, in the Archaebacteria [kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_(biology) "Kingdom (biology)")), but this term has fallen out of use.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-4) Archaeal cells have unique properties separating them from [Bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria") and [Eukaryota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryote "Eukaryote"), including: cell membranes made of ether-linked lipids; metabolisms such as methanogenesis; and a unique motility structure known as an archaellum.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-5)Archaea are further divided into multiple recognized [phyla](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylum "Phylum"). Classification is difficult because most have not been [isolated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isolation_(microbiology) "Isolation (microbiology)") in a laboratory and have been detected only by their [gene sequences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene "Gene") in environmental samples. It is unknown if they can produce [endospores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endospore "Endospore").

Archaea are often similar to bacteria in size and shape, although a few have very different shapes, such as the flat, square cells of _[Haloquadratum walsbyi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloquadratum\_walsbyi "Haloquadratum walsbyi")_.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-6) Despite this, archaea possess [genes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene "Gene") and several [metabolic pathways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolic_pathway "Metabolic pathway") that are more closely related to those of eukaryotes, notably for the [enzymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enzyme "Enzyme") involved in [transcription](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcription_(genetics) "Transcription (genetics)") and [translation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Translation_(biology) "Translation (biology)"). Other aspects of archaeal biochemistry are unique, such as their reliance on [ether lipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ether_lipid "Ether lipid") in their [cell membranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_membrane "Cell membrane"),[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-7) including [archaeols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaeol "Archaeol"). Archaea use more diverse energy sources than eukaryotes, ranging from [organic compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organic_compounds "Organic compounds") such as sugars, to [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia"), [metal ions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ion "Ion") or even [hydrogen gas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen"). The [salt-tolerant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halophile "Halophile")[Haloarchaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloarchaea "Haloarchaea") use sunlight as an energy source, and other species of archaea [fix carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fixation "Carbon fixation") (autotrophy), but unlike [cyanobacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanobacteria "Cyanobacteria"), no known species of archaea does both. Archaea [reproduce asexually](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asexual_reproduction "Asexual reproduction") by [binary fission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binary_fission "Binary fission"), [fragmentation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fragmentation_(reproduction) "Fragmentation (reproduction)"), or [budding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Budding "Budding"); unlike bacteria, no known species of Archaea form [endospores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endospore "Endospore"). The first observed archaea were [extremophiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extremophile "Extremophile"), living in extreme environments such as [hot springs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hot_spring "Hot spring") and [salt lakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt_lake "Salt lake") with no other organisms. Improved molecular detection tools led to the discovery of archaea in almost every [habitat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitat "Habitat"), including soil,[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Chow_2022-8) oceans, and [marshlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshland "Marshland"). Archaea are particularly numerous in the oceans, and the archaea in [plankton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plankton "Plankton") may be one of the most abundant groups of organisms on the planet.

Archaea are a major part of [Earth's life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life "Life"). They are part of the [microbiota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microbiota "Microbiota") of all organisms. In the [human microbiome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_microbiome "Human microbiome"), they are important in the [gut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colon_(anatomy) "Colon (anatomy)"), mouth, and on the skin.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Bang2015-9) Their morphological, metabolic, and geographical diversity permits them to play multiple ecological roles: carbon fixation; [nitrogen cycling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle "Nitrogen cycle"); organic compound turnover; and maintaining microbial [symbiotic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbiosis "Symbiosis") and [syntrophic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syntrophy "Syntrophy") communities, for example.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Chow_2022-8)[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-10) No archaea are known to be [pathogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathogen "Pathogen") or [parasites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parasite "Parasite"); many are [mutualists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutualism_(biology) "Mutualism (biology)") or [commensals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commensalism "Commensalism"), such as the [methanogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen") (methane-producers) that inhabit the [gastrointestinal tract](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gastrointestinal_tract "Gastrointestinal tract") in humans and [ruminants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruminant "Ruminant"), where their vast numbers facilitate [digestion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digestion "Digestion"). Methanogens are used in [biogas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogas "Biogas") production and [sewage treatment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sewage_treatment "Sewage treatment"), while [biotechnology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biotechnology "Biotechnology") exploits enzymes from extremophile archaea that can endure high temperatures and organic [solvents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solvents "Solvents").

Discovery and classification
----------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Discovery and classification")]

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Aerial_image_of_Grand_Prismatic_Spring_%28view_from_the_south%29.jpg/250px-Aerial_image_of_Grand_Prismatic_Spring_%28view_from_the_south%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Aerial_image_of_Grand_Prismatic_Spring_(view_from_the_south).jpg)

Archaea were discovered in volcanic [hot springs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hot_spring "Hot spring"). Pictured here is [Grand Prismatic Spring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grand_Prismatic_Spring "Grand Prismatic Spring") of [Yellowstone National Park](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park "Yellowstone National Park").

For much of the 20th century, prokaryotes were regarded as a single group of organisms and classified based on their [biochemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biochemistry "Biochemistry"), [morphology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morphology_(biology) "Morphology (biology)") and [metabolism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolism "Metabolism"). Microbiologists tried to classify microorganisms based on the structures of their [cell walls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_wall "Cell wall"), their shapes, and the substances they consume.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-11) In 1965, [Emile Zuckerkandl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emile_Zuckerkandl "Emile Zuckerkandl") and [Linus Pauling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linus_Pauling "Linus Pauling")[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-12) instead proposed using the sequences of the [genes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene "Gene") in different prokaryotes to work out how they are related to each other. This [phylogenetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylogenetics "Phylogenetics") approach is the main method used today.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid30148503-13)

Archaea were first classified separately from bacteria in 1977 by [Carl Woese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Woese "Carl Woese") and [George E. Fox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_E._Fox "George E. Fox"), based on their [ribosomal RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA "Ribosomal RNA") (rRNA) genes.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceA-14) (At that time only the [methanogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen") were known). They called these groups the _Urkingdoms_ of Archaebacteria and Eubacteria, though other researchers treated them as [kingdoms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_(biology) "Kingdom (biology)") or subkingdoms. Woese and Fox gave the first evidence for Archaebacteria as a separate "line of descent": 1. lack of [peptidoglycan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peptidoglycan "Peptidoglycan") in their cell walls, 2. two unusual coenzymes, 3. results of [16S ribosomal RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/16S_ribosomal_RNA "16S ribosomal RNA") gene sequencing. To emphasize this difference, Woese, [Otto Kandler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otto_Kandler "Otto Kandler") and [Mark Wheelis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Wheelis "Mark Wheelis") later proposed reclassifying organisms into three then thought to be natural [domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_(biology) "Domain (biology)") known as the [three-domain system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Three-domain_system "Three-domain system"): the [Eukarya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukarya "Eukarya"), the [Bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria") and the Archaea,[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Woese-15) in what is now known as the [Woesian Revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Woeseian_revolution "Woeseian revolution").[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-16)

The word _archaea_ comes from the [Ancient Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek "Ancient Greek")[ἀρχαῖα](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CF%81%CF%87%CE%B1%E1%BF%96%CE%BF%CF%82 "wikt:ἀρχαῖος"), meaning "ancient things",[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-17) as the first representatives of the domain Archaea were [methanogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen") and it was assumed that their metabolism reflected Earth's primitive atmosphere and the organisms' antiquity, but as new habitats were studied, more organisms were discovered. Extreme [halophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halophilic "Halophilic")[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Magrum-18) and [hyperthermophilic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperthermophilic "Hyperthermophilic") microbes[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Stetter-19) were also included in Archaea. For a long time, archaea were seen as extremophiles that exist only in extreme habitats such as [hot springs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hot_spring "Hot spring") and [salt lakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt_lake "Salt lake"), but by the end of the 20th century, archaea had been identified in non-extreme environments as well. Today, they are known to be a large and diverse group of organisms abundantly distributed throughout nature.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-DeLong-20) This new appreciation of the importance and ubiquity of archaea came from using [polymerase chain reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction "Polymerase chain reaction") (PCR) to detect prokaryotes from environmental samples (such as water or soil) by multiplying their ribosomal genes. This allows the detection and identification of organisms that have not been [cultured](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microbiological_culture "Microbiological culture") in the laboratory.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-21)[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-22)

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg/250px-Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Rio_tinto_river_CarolStoker_NASA_Ames_Research_Center.jpg)

The [ARMAN](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaeal_Richmond_Mine_acidophilic_nanoorganisms "Archaeal Richmond Mine acidophilic nanoorganisms") are a group of archaea discovered in [acid mine drainage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid_mine_drainage "Acid mine drainage") in the early 2000s.

The classification of archaea, and of prokaryotes in general, is a rapidly moving and contentious field. Current classification systems aim to organize archaea into groups of organisms that share structural features and common ancestors.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gevers-23) These classifications rely heavily on the use of the sequence of [ribosomal RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA "Ribosomal RNA") genes to reveal relationships among organisms ([molecular phylogenetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_phylogeny "Molecular phylogeny")).[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Robertson-24) Most of the culturable and well-investigated species of archaea are members of two main kingdoms, the [Methanobacteriati](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanobacteriati "Methanobacteriati") and the [Thermoproteati](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoproteati "Thermoproteati") (formerly TACK). Other groups have been tentatively created, such as the peculiar species _[Nanoarchaeum equitans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoarchaeum\_equitans "Nanoarchaeum equitans")_ — discovered in 2003 and assigned its own phylum, the Nanoarchaeota (reassigned to [Nanobdellota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanobdellota "Nanobdellota") in 2023[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-25)).[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-26) A new phylum "[_Candidatus_ Korarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korarchaeota "Korarchaeota")" (now [Thermoproteota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoproteota "Thermoproteota"))[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-:1-3) has also been proposed, containing a small group of unusual thermophilic species sharing features of both the main phyla.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-27)[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-28) Other detected species of archaea are only distantly related to any of these groups, such as the [Archaeal Richmond Mine acidophilic nanoorganisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaeal_Richmond_Mine_acidophilic_nanoorganisms "Archaeal Richmond Mine acidophilic nanoorganisms") (ARMAN, comprising [Micrarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micrarchaeota "Micrarchaeota") and Parvarchaeota), which were discovered in 2006[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-29) and are some of the smallest organisms known.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-30)

A [superphylum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylum "Phylum") – "TACK" (now kingdom [Thermoproteati](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoproteati "Thermoproteati"))– which includes the Thaumarchaeota (now [Nitrososphaerota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrososphaerota "Nitrososphaerota")), "[Augarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augarchaeota "Augarchaeota")", Crenarchaeota (now Thermoproteota), and "_[Candidatus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Candidatus "Candidatus")_ Korarchaeota" (now Thermoproteota)[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-:1-3) was proposed in 2011 to be related to the origin of eukaryotes.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Guy2011-31) In 2017, the newly discovered and newly named "Asgard" (now kingdom [Promethearchaeati](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asgard_(Archaea) "Asgard (Archaea)")) superphylum was proposed to be more closely related to the original eukaryote and a sister group to Thermoproteati / "TACK".[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ZN_Asgard2017-32)

In 2013, the superphylum "DPANN" (now kingdom Nanobdellati) was proposed to group "Nanoarchaeota", "[Nanohaloarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanohaloarchaeota "Nanohaloarchaeota")", [Archaeal Richmond Mine acidophilic nanoorganisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaeal_Richmond_Mine_acidophilic_nanoorganisms "Archaeal Richmond Mine acidophilic nanoorganisms") (ARMAN, comprising "[Micrarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micrarchaeota "Micrarchaeota")" and "[Parvarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parvarchaeota "Parvarchaeota")"), and other similar archaea. This archaeal superphylum encompasses at least 10 different lineages and includes organisms with extremely small cell and genome sizes and limited metabolic capabilities. Therefore, Nanobdellati/"DPANN" may include members obligately dependent on symbiotic interactions, and may even include novel parasites. However, other phylogenetic analyses found that Nanobdellati/"DPANN" does not form a [monophyletic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monophyly "Monophyly") group, and that the apparent grouping is caused by [long branch attraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Long_branch_attraction "Long branch attraction") (LBA), suggesting that all these lineages belong to Methanobacteriati.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-33)[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Petitjean-34)

According to Tom A. Williams _et al._ 2017,[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-WT2017-35) Castelle & Banfield (2018)[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-CCBJ2018-36) and [GTDB](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GTDB "GTDB") release 10-RS226 (16th April 2025).[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-about-37)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-tree-38)[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-taxon_history-39)

The classification of archaea into species is also controversial. [Ernst Mayr](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Mayr "Ernst Mayr")'s [species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species "Species") definition — a [reproductively isolated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reproductive_isolation "Reproductive isolation") group of interbreeding organisms — does not apply, as archaea reproduce only asexually.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-41)

Archaea show high levels of [horizontal gene transfer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer "Horizontal gene transfer") between lineages. Some researchers suggest that individuals can be grouped into species-like populations given highly similar genomes and infrequent gene transfer to/from cells with less-related genomes, as in the genus _[Ferroplasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferroplasma "Ferroplasma")_.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-42) On the other hand, studies in _[Halorubrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halorubrum "Halorubrum")_ found significant genetic transfer to/from less-related populations, limiting the criterion's applicability.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-43) Some researchers question whether such species designations have practical meaning.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-44)

Current knowledge on genetic diversity in archaeans is fragmentary, so the total number of species cannot be estimated with any accuracy.[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Robertson-24) Estimates of the number of phyla range from 18 to 23, of which only 8 have representatives that have been cultured and studied directly. Many of these hypothesized groups are known from a single rRNA sequence, so the level of diversity remains obscure.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-45) This situation is also seen in the Bacteria; many uncultured microbes present similar issues with characterization.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-46)

The following phyla have been validly published according to the [Prokaryotic Code](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Code_of_Nomenclature_of_Prokaryotes "International Code of Nomenclature of Prokaryotes"); belonging to the four kingdoms of archaea:[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-47)[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-48)[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-:1-3)

*   [Methanobacteriota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanobacteriota "Methanobacteriota")
*   [Microcaldota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microcaldota "Microcaldota")
*   [Nanobdellota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoarchaeota "Nanoarchaeota")
*   [Promethearchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asgard_(Archaea) "Asgard (Archaea)")
*   [Thermoproteota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoproteota "Thermoproteota")

The following phyla have been proposed, but have not been validly published according to the Prokaryotic Code; phyla that do not belong to any kingdom are shown in **bold**:[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-49)[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-:1-3)

Origin and evolution
--------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Origin and evolution")]

The [age of the Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth "Age of the Earth") is about 4.54 billion years.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-USGS1997-50)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-51)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-52) Scientific evidence suggests that [life began](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abiogenesis "Abiogenesis") on Earth at least 3.5[billion years ago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Billion_years_ago "Billion years ago").[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-AmSci-53)[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-AT-20120904-54) The earliest evidence for [life on Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life "Life") is [graphite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graphite "Graphite") found to be [biogenic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogenic_substance "Biogenic substance") in 3.7-billion-year-old [metasedimentary rocks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metasediment "Metasediment") discovered in [Western Greenland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Greenland "Western Greenland")[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-NG-20131208-55) and [microbial mat](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microbial_mat "Microbial mat")[fossils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossils "Fossils") found in 3.48-billion-year-old [sandstone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sandstone "Sandstone") discovered in [Western Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Australia "Western Australia").[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-AP-20131113-56)[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-AST-20131108-57) In 2015, possible remains of [biotic matter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biotic_material "Biotic material") were found in 4.1-billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-AP-20151019-58)[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-59)

Although probable prokaryotic cell [fossils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossils "Fossils") date to almost 3.5[billion years ago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bya "Bya"), most prokaryotes do not have distinctive morphologies, and fossil shapes cannot be used to identify them as archaea.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-60) Instead, [chemical fossils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_fossil "Chemical fossil") of unique [lipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipid "Lipid") are more informative because such compounds do not occur in other organisms.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-61) Some publications suggest that archaeal or eukaryotic lipid remains are present in [shales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shale "Shale") dating from 2.7 billion years ago,[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-62) though such data have since been questioned.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-63) These lipids have also been detected in even older rocks from west [Greenland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenland "Greenland"). The oldest such traces come from the [Isua district](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isua_greenstone_belt "Isua greenstone belt"), which includes Earth's oldest known sediments, formed 3.8 billion years ago.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-64) The archaeal lineage may be the most ancient that exists on Earth.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Wang-65)

Woese argued that the bacteria, archaea, and eukaryotes represent separate lines of descent that diverged early on from an ancestral colony of organisms.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-66)[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Woese1-67) One possibility[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Woese1-67)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Kandler-68) is that this occurred before the [evolution of cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution_of_cells "Evolution of cells"), when the lack of a typical cell membrane allowed unrestricted [lateral gene transfer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lateral_gene_transfer "Lateral gene transfer"), and that the common ancestors of the three domains arose by fixation of specific subsets of genes.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Woese1-67)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Kandler-68) It is possible that the [last common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") of bacteria and archaea was a [thermophile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermophile "Thermophile"), which raises the possibility that lower temperatures are "extreme environments" for archaea, and organisms that live in cooler environments appeared only later.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-69) Since archaea and bacteria are no more related to each other than they are to eukaryotes, the term _prokaryote_ may suggest a false similarity between them.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-PMID8177167-70) However, structural and functional similarities between lineages often occur because of shared ancestral traits or [evolutionary convergence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_evolution "Convergent evolution"). These similarities are known as a _grade_, and [prokaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prokaryote "Prokaryote") are best thought of as a grade of life, characterized by such features as an absence of membrane-bound organelles.

### Comparison with other domains

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Comparison with other domains")]

The following table compares some major characteristics of the three domains, to illustrate their similarities and differences.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-71)

Archaea were split off as a third domain because of the large differences in their ribosomal RNA structure. The particular molecule [16S rRNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/16S_rRNA "16S rRNA") is key to the production of proteins in all organisms. Because this function is so central to life, organisms with mutations in their 16S rRNA are unlikely to survive, leading to great (but not absolute) stability in the structure of this polynucleotide over generations. 16S rRNA is large enough to show organism-specific variations, but still small enough to be compared quickly. In 1977, Carl Woese, a microbiologist studying the genetic sequences of organisms, developed a new comparison method that involved splitting the RNA into fragments that could be sorted and compared with other fragments from other organisms.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceA-14) The more similar the patterns between species, the more closely they are related.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-74)

Woese used his new rRNA comparison method to categorize and contrast different organisms. He compared a variety of species and happened upon a group of methanogens with rRNA vastly different from any known prokaryotes or eukaryotes.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceA-14) These methanogens were much more similar to each other than to other organisms, leading Woese to propose the new domain of Archaea.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceA-14) His experiments showed that the archaea were genetically more similar to eukaryotes than prokaryotes, even though they were more similar to prokaryotes in structure.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-nature.com-75) This led to the conclusion that Archaea and Eukarya shared a common ancestor more recent than Eukarya and Bacteria.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-nature.com-75) The development of the nucleus occurred after the split between Bacteria and this common ancestor.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-nature.com-75)[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Woese-15)

One property unique to archaea is the abundant use of ether-linked lipids in their cell membranes. Ether linkages are more chemically stable than the ester linkages found in bacteria and eukarya, which may be a contributing factor to the ability of many archaea to survive in extreme environments that place heavy stress on cell membranes, such as extreme heat and [salinity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salinity "Salinity"). Comparative analysis of archaeal genomes has also identified several molecular [conserved signature indels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conserved_signature_indels "Conserved signature indels") and signature proteins uniquely present in either all archaea or different main groups within archaea.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-76)[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-77)[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-78) Another unique feature of archaea, found in no other organisms, is [methanogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogenesis "Methanogenesis") (the metabolic production of methane). Methanogenic archaea play a pivotal role in ecosystems with organisms that derive energy from oxidation of methane, many of which are bacteria, as they are often a major source of methane in such environments and can play a role as primary producers. [Methanogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen") also play a critical role in the [carbon cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_cycle "Carbon cycle"), breaking down organic carbon into methane, which is also a major greenhouse gas.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-79)

This difference in the biochemical structure of Bacteria and Archaea has been explained by researchers through evolutionary processes.[_[vague](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness "Wikipedia:Vagueness")_] It is theorized that both [domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domain_(biology) "Domain (biology)") originated at deep sea alkaline [hydrothermal vents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent_microbial_communities "Hydrothermal vent microbial communities"). At least twice, microbes evolved lipid biosynthesis and cell wall biochemistry. It has been suggested that the [last universal common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor "Last universal common ancestor") was a non-free-living organism.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-80) It may have had a permeable membrane composed of bacterial simple chain amphiphiles (fatty acids), including archaeal simple chain amphiphiles (isoprenoids). These stabilize fatty acid membranes in seawater; this property may have driven the divergence of bacterial and archaeal membranes, "with the later biosynthesis of phospholipids giving rise to the unique G1P and G3P headgroups of archaea and bacteria respectively. If so, the properties conferred by membrane isoprenoids place the lipid divide as early as the origin of life".[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-81)

### Relationship to bacteria

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Relationship to bacteria")]

![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png/500px-Collapsed_tree_labels_simplified.png)

[Phylogenetic tree](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree "Phylogenetic tree") showing the relationship between the Archaea and other domains of life. [Eukaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryote "Eukaryote") are colored red, archaea green and [bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria") blue. Adapted from [Ciccarelli et al. (2006)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#CITEREFCiccarelliDoerksvon_MeringCreevey2006)[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Cicc06-82)

The relationships among the [three domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Three-domain_system "Three-domain system") are of central importance for understanding the origin of life. Most of the [metabolic pathways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolic_pathway "Metabolic pathway"), which are the object of the majority of an organism's genes, are common between Archaea and Bacteria, while most genes involved in [gene expression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_expression "Gene expression") are common between Archaea and Eukarya.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-83) Within prokaryotes, archaeal cell structure is most similar to that of [Gram-positive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gram-positive "Gram-positive") bacteria, largely because both have a single lipid bilayer[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-1-84) and usually contain a thick sacculus (exoskeleton) of varying chemical composition.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-85) In some phylogenetic trees based upon different gene / protein sequences of prokaryotic [homologs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homology_(biology) "Homology (biology)"), the archaeal homologs are more closely related to those of gram-positive bacteria.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-1-84) Archaea and gram-positive bacteria also share conserved [indels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indels "Indels") in a number of important proteins, such as [Hsp70](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hsp70 "Hsp70") and [glutamine synthetase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glutamine_synthetase "Glutamine synthetase")I;[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-1-84)[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-2-86) but the phylogeny of these genes was interpreted to reveal inter-domain gene transfer,[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-87)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-88) and might not reflect the organismal relationship(s).[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid26323756-89)

It has been proposed that the archaea evolved from Gram-positive bacteria in response to antibiotic [selection pressure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_pressure "Evolutionary pressure").[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-1-84)[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-2-86)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-3-90) This is suggested by the observation that archaea are resistant to a wide variety of antibiotics that are produced primarily by Gram-positive bacteria,[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-1-84)[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-2-86) and that these antibiotics act primarily on the genes that distinguish archaea from bacteria. The proposal is that the selective pressure towards resistance generated by the gram-positive antibiotics was eventually sufficient to cause extensive changes in many of the antibiotics' target genes, and that these strains represented the common ancestors of present-day Archaea.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-3-90) The evolution of Archaea in response to antibiotic selection, or any other competitive selective pressure, could also explain their adaptation to extreme environments (such as high temperature or acidity) as the result of a search for unoccupied niches to escape from antibiotic-producing organisms;[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gupta-3-90)[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-91)[Cavalier-Smith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Cavalier-Smith "Thomas Cavalier-Smith") has made a similar suggestion, the [Neomura](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neomura "Neomura") hypothesis.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-92) This proposal is also supported by other work investigating protein structural relationships[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-93) and studies that suggest that gram-positive bacteria may constitute the earliest branching lineages within the prokaryotes.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-94)

### Relation to eukaryotes

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Relation to eukaryotes")]

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Symbiogenesis_2_mergers.svg/500px-Symbiogenesis_2_mergers.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Symbiogenesis_2_mergers.svg)

In the theory of [symbiogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbiogenesis "Symbiogenesis"), a merger of an Promethearchaeati / "Asgard" archaean and an aerobic bacterium created the [eukaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryote "Eukaryote"), with aerobic [mitochondria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondrion "Mitochondrion"); a second merger added [chloroplasts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chloroplast "Chloroplast"), creating the [green plants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viridiplantae "Viridiplantae").[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-latorre-95)

The evolutionary relationship between archaea and [eukaryotes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryote "Eukaryote") remains unclear. Aside from the similarities in cell structure and function that are discussed below, many genetic trees group the two.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid29123225-96)

Complicating factors include claims that the relationship between eukaryotes and the archaeal phylum [Thermoproteota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoproteota "Thermoproteota") is closer than the relationship between the [Methanobacteriati](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanobacteriati "Methanobacteriati") and the phylum Thermoproteota[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceC-97) and the presence of archaea-like genes in certain bacteria, such as _[Thermotoga maritima](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermotoga\_maritima "Thermotoga maritima")_, from [horizontal gene transfer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer "Horizontal gene transfer").[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-98) The standard hypothesis states that the ancestor of the eukaryotes diverged early from the Archaea,[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-99)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-100) and that eukaryotes arose through [symbiogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbiogenesis "Symbiogenesis"), the fusion of an archaean and a eubacterium, which formed the [mitochondria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondrion "Mitochondrion"); this hypothesis explains the genetic similarities between the groups.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-latorre-95) The [eocyte hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eocyte_hypothesis "Eocyte hypothesis") instead posits that [Eukaryota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryota "Eukaryota") emerged relatively late from the Archaea.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-2domains-101)

A lineage of archaea discovered in 2015, _[Lokiarchaeum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lokiarchaeum "Lokiarchaeum")_ (of the proposed new phylum "[Lokiarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lokiarchaeota "Lokiarchaeota")"), named for a [hydrothermal vent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent "Hydrothermal vent") called [Loki's Castle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loki%27s_Castle "Loki's Castle") in the Arctic Ocean, was found to be the most closely related to eukaryotes known at that time. It has been called a transitional organism between prokaryotes and eukaryotes.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-NYT-20150506-102)[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-103)

Several sister phyla of "Lokiarchaeota" have since been found ("[Thorarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thorarchaeota "Thorarchaeota")", "[Odinarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odinarchaeota "Odinarchaeota")", "[Heimdallarchaeota](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Heimdallarchaeota&action=edit&redlink=1 "Heimdallarchaeota (page does not exist)")"), all together comprising a newly proposed [supergroup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supergroup_(biology) "Supergroup (biology)") "Asgard".[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ZN_Asgard2017-32)[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-NCBI_taxonomy_page_on_Archaea-104)[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-105)

Details of the relation of Promethearchaeati / "Asgard" members and eukaryotes are still under consideration,[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid31384702-106) although, in January 2020, scientists reported that _[Promethearchaeum syntrophicum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Promethearchaeum\_syntrophicum "Promethearchaeum syntrophicum")_, a type of Promethearchaeati / "Asgard" archaea, may be a possible link between simple [prokaryotic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prokaryotic "Prokaryotic") and complex [eukaryotic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eukaryotic "Eukaryotic") microorganisms about two billion years ago.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-NYT-2020115-107)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-NAT-20200115-108)[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ARC-200929-109)

Individual archaea range from 0.1[micrometers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micrometre "Micrometre") (μm) to over 15 μm in diameter, and occur in various shapes, commonly as spheres, rods, spirals or plates.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Bergey-110) Other morphologies in the [Thermoproteota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoproteota "Thermoproteota") include irregularly shaped lobed cells in _[Sulfolobus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobus "Sulfolobus")_, needle-like filaments that are less than half a micrometer in diameter in _[Thermofilum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermofilum "Thermofilum")_, and almost perfectly rectangular rods in _[Thermoproteus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoproteus "Thermoproteus")_ and _[Pyrobaculum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrobaculum "Pyrobaculum")_.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-111) Archaea in the genus _[Haloquadratum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloquadratum "Haloquadratum")_ such as _[Haloquadratum walsbyi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloquadratum\_walsbyi "Haloquadratum walsbyi")_ are flat, square specimens that live in hypersaline pools.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Walsby1980-112) These unusual shapes are probably maintained by both their cell walls and a [prokaryotic cytoskeleton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prokaryotic_cytoskeleton "Prokaryotic cytoskeleton"). Proteins related to the cytoskeleton components of other organisms exist in archaea,[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-113) and filaments form within their cells,[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-114) but in contrast with other organisms, these cellular structures are poorly understood.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-115) In _[Thermoplasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoplasma "Thermoplasma")_ and _[Ferroplasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferroplasma "Ferroplasma")_ the lack of a [cell wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_wall "Cell wall") means that the cells have irregular shapes, and can resemble [amoebae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amoeboid "Amoeboid").[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Golyshina-116)

Some species form aggregates or filaments of cells up to 200 μm long.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Bergey-110) These organisms can be prominent in [biofilms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biofilm "Biofilm").[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-117) Notably, aggregates of _[Thermococcus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermococcus "Thermococcus") coalescens_ cells fuse together in culture, forming single giant cells.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-118) Archaea in the genus _[Pyrodictium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrodictium "Pyrodictium")_ produce an elaborate multicell colony involving arrays of long, thin hollow tubes called _cannulae_ that stick out from the cells' surfaces and connect them into a dense bush-like agglomeration.[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-119) The function of these cannulae is not settled, but they may allow communication or nutrient exchange with neighbors.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-120) Multi-species colonies exist, such as the "string-of-pearls" community that was discovered in 2001 in a German swamp. Round whitish colonies of a novel Methanobacteriati species are spaced along thin filaments that can range up to 15 centimetres (5.9 in) long; these filaments are made of a particular bacteria species.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-121)

Structure, composition development, and operation
-------------------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Structure, composition development, and operation")]

Archaea and bacteria have generally similar [cell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_(biology) "Cell (biology)") structure, but cell composition and organization set the archaea apart. Like bacteria, archaea lack interior membranes and [organelles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organelle "Organelle").[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-PMID8177167-70) Like bacteria, the [cell membranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_membrane "Cell membrane") of archaea are usually bounded by a [cell wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_wall "Cell wall") and they swim using one or more [flagella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flagella "Flagella").[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Thomas-122) Structurally, archaea are most similar to [gram-positive bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gram-positive_bacteria "Gram-positive bacteria"). Most have a single plasma membrane and cell wall, and lack a [periplasmic space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Periplasmic_space "Periplasmic space"); the exception to this general rule is _[Ignicoccus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ignicoccus "Ignicoccus")_, which possess a particularly large periplasm that contains membrane-bound [vesicles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vesicle_(biology) "Vesicle (biology)") and is enclosed by an outer membrane.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-123)

### Cell wall and archaella

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Cell wall and archaella")]

Most archaea (but not _[Thermoplasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoplasma "Thermoplasma")_ and _[Ferroplasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferroplasma "Ferroplasma")_) possess a cell wall.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Golyshina-116) In most archaea, the wall is assembled from surface-layer proteins, which form an [S-layer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S-layer "S-layer").[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-124) An S-layer is a rigid array of protein molecules that cover the outside of the cell (like [chain mail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chain_mail "Chain mail")).[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-125) This layer provides both chemical and physical protection, and can prevent [macromolecules](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macromolecule "Macromolecule") from contacting the cell membrane.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Kandler1998-126) Unlike bacteria, archaea lack [peptidoglycan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peptidoglycan "Peptidoglycan") in their cell walls.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Howland-127)[Methanobacteriales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanobacteriales "Methanobacteriales") do have cell walls containing [pseudopeptidoglycan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudopeptidoglycan "Pseudopeptidoglycan"), which resembles eubacterial peptidoglycan in morphology, function, and physical structure, but pseudopeptidoglycan is distinct in chemical structure; it lacks [D-amino acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D-amino_acids "D-amino acids") and [N-acetylmuramic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N-acetylmuramic_acid "N-acetylmuramic acid"), substituting the latter with [N-Acetyltalosaminuronic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N-Acetyltalosaminuronic_acid "N-Acetyltalosaminuronic acid").[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Kandler1998-126)

Archaeal flagella are known as [archaella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaellum "Archaellum"), that operate like bacterial [flagella](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flagellum "Flagellum") – their long stalks are driven by rotatory motors at the base. These motors are powered by a [proton gradient](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient "Electrochemical gradient") across the membrane, but archaella are notably different in composition and development.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Thomas-122) The two types of flagella evolved from different ancestors. The bacterial flagellum shares a common ancestor with the [type III secretion system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Type_III_secretion_system "Type III secretion system"),[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-128)[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-129) while archaeal flagella appear to have evolved from bacterial [type IV pili](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Type_IV_pili "Type IV pili").[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-130) In contrast with the bacterial flagellum, which is hollow and assembled by subunits moving up the central pore to the tip of the flagella, archaeal flagella are synthesized by adding subunits at the base.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-131)

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Archaea_membrane.svg/380px-Archaea_membrane.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Archaea_membrane.svg)

Membrane structures. **Top**,an archaeal phospholipid: **1**,isoprene chains; **2**,ether linkages; **3**,[L-glycerol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levorotation_and_dextrorotation "Levorotation and dextrorotation")[moiety](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moiety_(chemistry) "Moiety (chemistry)"); **4**,phosphate group. **Middle**,a bacterial or eukaryotic phospholipid: **5**,fatty acid chains; **6**,ester linkages; **7**,[D-glycerol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levorotation_and_dextrorotation "Levorotation and dextrorotation") moiety; **8**,phosphate group. **Bottom**: **9**,lipid bilayer of bacteria and eukaryotes; **10**,lipid monolayer of some archaea.

Archaeal membranes are made of molecules that are distinctly different from those in all other life forms, showing that archaea are related only distantly to bacteria and eukaryotes.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Koga-132) In all organisms, [cell membranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_membrane "Cell membrane") are made of molecules known as [phospholipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phospholipid "Phospholipid"). These molecules possess both a [polar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_polarity "Chemical polarity") part that dissolves in water (the [phosphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phosphate "Phosphate") "head"), and a "greasy" non-polar part that does not (the lipid tail). These dissimilar parts are connected by a [glycerol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glycerol "Glycerol") moiety. In water, phospholipids cluster, with the heads facing the water and the tails facing away from it. The major structure in cell membranes is a double layer of these phospholipids, which is called a [lipid bilayer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipid_bilayer "Lipid bilayer").[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid29739208-133)

The phospholipids of archaea are unusual in four ways:

*   They have membranes composed of glycerol-[ether lipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ether_lipid "Ether lipid"), whereas bacteria and eukaryotes have membranes composed mainly of glycerol-[ester](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ester "Ester")[lipids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipid "Lipid").[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-134) The difference is the type of bond that joins the lipids to the glycerol moiety; the two types are shown in yellow in the figure at the right. In ester lipids, this is an [ester bond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ester "Ester"), whereas in ether lipids this is an [ether bond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ether "Ether").[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid25229144-135)
*   The [stereochemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stereochemistry "Stereochemistry") of the archaeal glycerol moiety is the mirror image of that found in other organisms. The glycerol moiety can occur in two forms that are mirror images of one another, called _[enantiomers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enantiomer "Enantiomer")_. Just as a right hand does not fit easily into a left-handed glove, enantiomers of one type generally cannot be used or made by [enzymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enzyme "Enzyme") adapted for the other. The archaeal phospholipids are built on a backbone of _sn_-glycerol-1-phosphate, which is an enantiomer of _sn_-glycerol-3-phosphate, the phospholipid backbone found in bacteria and eukaryotes. This suggests that archaea use entirely different enzymes for synthesizing phospholipids as compared to bacteria and eukaryotes. Such enzymes developed very early in life's history, indicating an early split from the other two domains.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Koga-132)
*   Archaeal lipid tails differ from those of other organisms in that they are based upon long [isoprenoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isoprene "Isoprene") chains with multiple side-branches, sometimes with [cyclopropane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclopropane "Cyclopropane") or [cyclohexane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclohexane "Cyclohexane") rings.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-136) By contrast, the [fatty acids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fatty_acid "Fatty acid") in the membranes of other organisms have straight chains without side branches or rings. Although isoprenoids play an important role in the biochemistry of many organisms, only the archaea use them to make phospholipids. These branched chains may help prevent archaeal membranes from leaking at high temperatures.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-137)
*   In some archaea, the lipid bilayer is replaced by a monolayer. In effect, the archaea fuse the tails of two phospholipid molecules into a single molecule with two polar heads (a [bolaamphiphile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolaamphiphile "Bolaamphiphile")); this fusion may make their membranes more rigid and better able to resist harsh environments.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-138) For example, the lipids in _[Ferroplasma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferroplasma "Ferroplasma")_ are of this type, which is thought to aid this organism's survival in its highly acidic habitat.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-139)

Archaea exhibit a great variety of chemical reactions in their [metabolism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolism "Metabolism") and use many sources of energy. These reactions are classified into [nutritional groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primary_nutritional_groups "Primary nutritional groups"), depending on energy and carbon sources. Some archaea obtain energy from [inorganic compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inorganic_compound "Inorganic compound") such as [sulfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur") or [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia") (they are [chemotrophs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemotroph "Chemotroph")). These include [nitrifiers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrifying_bacteria "Nitrifying bacteria"), [methanogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen") and [anaerobic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaerobic_organism "Anaerobic organism")[methane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methane "Methane")[oxidisers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxidiser "Oxidiser").[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-valentine-140) In these reactions, one compound passes electrons to another (in a [redox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Redox "Redox") reaction), releasing energy to fuel the cell's activities. One compound acts as an [electron donor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_donor "Electron donor") and one as an [electron acceptor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_acceptor "Electron acceptor"). The energy released is used to generate [adenosine triphosphate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate "Adenosine triphosphate") (ATP) through [chemiosmosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemiosmosis "Chemiosmosis"), the same basic process that happens in the [mitochondrion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondrion "Mitochondrion") of eukaryotic cells.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Schafer-141)

Other groups of archaea use sunlight as a source of energy (they are [phototrophs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phototroph "Phototroph")), but oxygen–generating [photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis "Photosynthesis") does not occur in any of these organisms.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Schafer-141) Many basic [metabolic pathways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolic_pathway "Metabolic pathway") are shared among all forms of life; for example, archaea use a modified form of [glycolysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glycolysis "Glycolysis") (the [Entner–Doudoroff pathway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entner%E2%80%93Doudoroff_pathway "Entner–Doudoroff pathway")) and either a complete or partial [citric acid cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle "Citric acid cycle").[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Zillig-142) These similarities to other organisms probably reflect both early origins in the history of life and their high level of efficiency.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-143)

Nutritional types in archaeal metabolism | Nutritional type | Source of energy | Source of carbon | Examples |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| [Phototrophs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phototroph "Phototroph") | Sunlight | Organic compounds | _[Halobacterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halobacterium "Halobacterium")_ |
| [Lithotrophs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithotroph "Lithotroph") | Inorganic compounds | Organic compounds or [carbon fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fixation "Carbon fixation") | _[Ferroglobus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferroglobus "Ferroglobus")_, _[Methanobacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanobacteria "Methanobacteria")_ or _[Pyrolobus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrolobus "Pyrolobus")_ |
| [Organotrophs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Organotroph "Organotroph") | Organic compounds | Organic compounds or [carbon fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fixation "Carbon fixation") | _[Pyrococcus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrococcus "Pyrococcus")_, _[Sulfolobus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobus "Sulfolobus")_ or _[Methanosarcinales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanosarcinales "Methanosarcinales")_ |

Some Methanobacteriati are [methanogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen") (archaea that produce methane as a result of metabolism) living in [anaerobic environments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaerobic_environment "Anaerobic environment"), such as swamps. This form of metabolism evolved early, and it is even possible that the first free-living organism was a methanogen.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-144) A common reaction involves the use of [carbon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_dioxide "Carbon dioxide") as an electron acceptor to oxidize [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen"). Methanogenesis involves a range of [coenzymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coenzyme "Coenzyme") that are unique to these archaea, such as [coenzyme M](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coenzyme_M "Coenzyme M") and [methanofuran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanofuran "Methanofuran").[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-145) Other organic compounds such as [alcohols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcohol_(chemistry) "Alcohol (chemistry)"), [acetic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acetic_acid "Acetic acid") or [formic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formic_acid "Formic acid") are used as alternative [electron acceptors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_acceptor "Electron acceptor") by methanogens. These reactions are common in [gut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gut_(zoology) "Gut (zoology)")-dwelling archaea. [Acetic acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acetic_acid "Acetic acid") is also broken down into methane and carbon dioxide directly, by _acetotrophic_ archaea. These acetotrophs are archaea in the order [Methanosarcinales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanosarcinales "Methanosarcinales"), and are a major part of the communities of microorganisms that produce [biogas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogas "Biogas").[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-146)

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Bacteriorhodopsin.png/250px-Bacteriorhodopsin.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bacteriorhodopsin.png)

Bacteriorhodopsin from _[Halobacterium salinarum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halobacterium\_salinarum "Halobacterium salinarum")_. The retinol [cofactor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry) "Cofactor (biochemistry)") and residues involved in proton transfer are shown as [ball-and-stick models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model "Ball-and-stick model").[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-147)

Other archaea use CO

2 in the [atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere "Atmosphere") as a source of carbon, in a process called [carbon fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fixation "Carbon fixation") (they are [autotrophs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autotroph "Autotroph")). This process involves either a highly modified form of the [Calvin cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calvin_cycle "Calvin cycle")[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-148) or another metabolic pathway called the 3-hydroxypropionate/ 4-hydroxybutyrate cycle.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-149) The Thermoproteota also use the [reverse Krebs cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reverse_Krebs_cycle "Reverse Krebs cycle") while the Methanobacteriati also use the [reductive acetyl-CoA pathway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reductive_acetyl-CoA_pathway "Reductive acetyl-CoA pathway").[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-150) Carbon fixation is powered by inorganic energy sources. No known archaea carry out [photosynthesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photosynthesis "Photosynthesis").[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Bryant-151) Archaeal energy sources are extremely diverse, and range from the oxidation of [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia") by the [Nitrosopumilales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrosopumilales "Nitrosopumilales")[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceD-152)[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceB-153) to the oxidation of [hydrogen sulfide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide "Hydrogen sulfide") or elemental [sulfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur") by species of _[Sulfolobus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobus "Sulfolobus")_, using either oxygen or metal ions as electron acceptors.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Schafer-141)

[Phototrophic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phototroph "Phototroph") archaea use light to produce chemical energy in the form of ATP. In the [Halobacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halobacteria "Halobacteria"), light-activated ion pumps like [bacteriorhodopsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteriorhodopsin "Bacteriorhodopsin") and [halorhodopsin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halorhodopsin "Halorhodopsin") generate ion gradients by pumping ions out of and into the cell across the [plasma membrane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plasma_membrane "Plasma membrane"). The energy stored in these [electrochemical gradients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient "Electrochemical gradient") is then converted into ATP by [ATP synthase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ATP_synthase "ATP synthase").[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Bergey-110) This process is a form of [photophosphorylation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photophosphorylation "Photophosphorylation"). The ability of these light-driven pumps to move ions across membranes depends on light-driven changes in the structure of a [retinol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retinol "Retinol")[cofactor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry) "Cofactor (biochemistry)") buried in the center of the protein.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-154)

Archaea usually have a single [circular chromosome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circular_chromosome "Circular chromosome"),[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Allers-155) but many euryarchaea have been shown to bear multiple copies of this chromosome.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-156) The largest known archaeal genome as of 2002 was 5,751,492[base pairs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_pair "Base pair") in _[Methanosarcina acetivorans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanosarcina\_acetivorans "Methanosarcina acetivorans")_.[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-157) The tiny 490,885 base-pair genome of _[Nanoarchaeum equitans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoarchaeum\_equitans "Nanoarchaeum equitans")_ is one-tenth of this size and the smallest archaeal genome known; it is estimated to contain only 537 protein-encoding genes.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pnas.org-158) Smaller independent pieces of DNA, called _[plasmids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plasmid "Plasmid")_, are also found in archaea. Plasmids may be transferred between cells by physical contact, in a process that may be similar to [bacterial conjugation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacterial_conjugation "Bacterial conjugation").[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-159)[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-SotaTop-160)

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/RT8-4.jpg/250px-RT8-4.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:RT8-4.jpg)

_[Sulfolobus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobus "Sulfolobus")_ infected with the [DNA virus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_virus "DNA virus")[STSV1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STSV-1 "STSV-1").[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-161) Bar is 1[micrometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Micrometre "Micrometre").

Archaea are genetically distinct from bacteria and eukaryotes, with up to 15% of the proteins encoded by any one archaeal genome being unique to the domain, although most of these unique genes have no known function.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-162) Of the remainder of the unique proteins that have an identified function, most belong to the Methanobacteriati and are involved in methanogenesis. The proteins that archaea, bacteria and eukaryotes share form a common core of cell function, relating mostly to [transcription](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcription_(genetics) "Transcription (genetics)"), [translation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Translation_(biology) "Translation (biology)"), and [nucleotide metabolism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleotide "Nucleotide").[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gaasterland-163) Other characteristic archaeal features are the organization of genes of related function – such as enzymes that catalyze steps in the same [metabolic pathway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolic_pathway "Metabolic pathway") into novel [operons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operon "Operon"), and large differences in [tRNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TRNA "TRNA") genes and their [aminoacyl tRNA synthetases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aminoacyl_tRNA_synthetase "Aminoacyl tRNA synthetase").[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Gaasterland-163)

Transcription in archaea more closely resembles eukaryotic than bacterial transcription, with the archaeal [RNA polymerase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_polymerase "RNA polymerase") being very close to its equivalent in eukaryotes,[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Allers-155) while archaeal translation shows signs of both bacterial and eukaryotic equivalents.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Dennis-164) Although archaea have only one type of RNA polymerase, its structure and function in transcription seems to be close to that of the eukaryotic [RNA polymerase II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_polymerase_II "RNA polymerase II"), with similar protein assemblies (the [general transcription factors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_transcription_factor "General transcription factor")) directing the binding of the RNA polymerase to a gene's [promoter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Promoter_(biology) "Promoter (biology)"),[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-165) but other archaeal [transcription factors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcription_factor "Transcription factor") are closer to those found in bacteria.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-166)[Post-transcriptional modification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post-transcriptional_modification "Post-transcriptional modification") is simpler than in eukaryotes, since most archaeal genes lack [introns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intron "Intron"), although there are many introns in their [transfer RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transfer_RNA "Transfer RNA") and [ribosomal RNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ribosomal_RNA "Ribosomal RNA") genes,[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-167) and introns may occur in a few protein-encoding genes.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-168)[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-169)

### Gene transfer and genetic exchange

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Gene transfer and genetic exchange")]

_[Haloferax volcanii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloferax\_volcanii "Haloferax volcanii")_, an extreme halophilic archaeon, forms cytoplasmic bridges between cells that appear to be used for transfer of DNA from one cell to another in either direction.[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-170)

When the hyperthermophilic archaea _[Sulfolobus solfataricus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobus\_solfataricus "Sulfolobus solfataricus")_[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Frols2008-171) and _[Sulfolobus acidocaldarius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobus\_acidocaldarius "Sulfolobus acidocaldarius")_[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Ajon-172) are exposed to DNA-damaging UV irradiation or to the agents [bleomycin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bleomycin "Bleomycin") or [mitomycin C](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitomycin_C "Mitomycin C"), species-specific cellular aggregation is induced. Aggregation in _S.solfataricus_ could not be induced by other physical stressors, such as pH or temperature shift,[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Frols2008-171) suggesting that aggregation is induced specifically by [DNA damage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_damage "DNA damage"). Ajon et al.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Ajon-172) showed that UV-induced cellular aggregation mediates chromosomal marker exchange with high frequency in _S.acidocaldarius_. Recombination rates exceeded those of uninduced cultures by up to three orders of magnitude. Frols et al.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Frols2008-171)[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-173) and Ajon et al.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Ajon-172) hypothesized that cellular aggregation enhances species-specific DNA transfer between _Sulfolobus_ cells in order to provide increased repair of damaged DNA by means of [homologous recombination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homologous_recombination "Homologous recombination"). This response may be a primitive form of sexual interaction similar to the more well-studied bacterial transformation systems that are also associated with species-specific DNA transfer between cells leading to homologous recombinational repair of DNA damage.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-174)

Archaea are the target of a number of [viruses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virus "Virus") in a diverse virosphere distinct from bacterial and eukaryotic viruses. They have been organized into 15–18 DNA-based families so far, but multiple species remain un-isolated and await classification.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-:0-175)[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Krupovic_2018-176)[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-177) These families can be informally divided into two groups: archaea-specific and cosmopolitan. Archaeal-specific viruses target only archaean species and currently include 12 families. Numerous unique, previously unidentified viral structures have been observed in this group, including: bottle-shaped, spindle-shaped, coil-shaped, and droplet-shaped viruses.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Krupovic_2018-176) While the reproductive cycles and genomic mechanisms of archaea-specific species may be similar to other viruses, they bear unique characteristics that were specifically developed due to the morphology of host cells they infect.[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-:0-175) Their virus release mechanisms differ from that of other phages. [Bacteriophages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteriophage "Bacteriophage") generally undergo either [lytic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lytic_cycle "Lytic cycle") pathways, [lysogenic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lysogenic_cycle "Lysogenic cycle") pathways, or (rarely) a mix of the two.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-178) Most archaea-specific viral strains maintain a stable, somewhat lysogenic, relationship with their hosts – appearing as a chronic infection. This involves the gradual, and continuous, production and release of [virions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virus "Virus") without killing the host cell.[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-179) Prangishyili (2013) noted that it has been hypothesized that tailed archaeal phages originated from bacteriophages capable of infecting [haloarchaeal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloarchaea "Haloarchaea") species. If the hypothesis is correct, it can be concluded that other [double-stranded DNA viruses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Double-stranded_DNA_viruses "Double-stranded DNA viruses") that make up the rest of the archaea-specific group are their own unique group in the global viral community. Krupovic et al. (2018) states that the high levels of [horizontal gene transfer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horizontal_gene_transfer "Horizontal gene transfer"), rapid mutation rates in viral genomes, and lack of universal gene sequences have led researchers to perceive the evolutionary pathway of archaeal viruses as a network.
