The lack of similarities among [phylogenetic markers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylogenetic_marker "Phylogenetic marker") in this network and the global virosphere, as well as external linkages to non-viral elements, may suggest that some species of archaea specific viruses evolved from non-viral [mobile genetic elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobile_genetic_elements "Mobile genetic elements") (MGE).[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Krupovic_2018-176)

These viruses have been studied in most detail in thermophilics, particularly the orders [Sulfolobales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobales "Sulfolobales") and [Thermoproteales](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermoproteales "Thermoproteales").[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-180) Two groups of [single-stranded DNA viruses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single-stranded_DNA_viruses "Single-stranded DNA viruses") that infect archaea have been recently isolated. One group is exemplified by the _[Halorubrum pleomorphic virus 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halorubrum\_pleomorphic\_virus\_1 "Halorubrum pleomorphic virus 1")_ (_[Pleolipoviridae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pleolipoviridae "Pleolipoviridae")_) infecting halophilic archaea,[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-181) and the other one by the _[Aeropyrum coil-shaped virus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aeropyrum\_coil-shaped\_virus "Aeropyrum coil-shaped virus")_ (_[Spiraviridae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiraviridae "Spiraviridae")_) infecting a hyperthermophilic (optimal growth at 90–95°C) host.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-182) Notably, the latter virus has the largest currently reported ssDNA genome. Defenses against these viruses may involve [RNA interference](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_interference "RNA interference") from [repetitive DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Repetitive_DNA "Repetitive DNA") sequences that are related to the genes of the viruses.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-183)[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-184)

Archaea reproduce asexually by binary or multiple [fission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fission_(biology) "Fission (biology)"), fragmentation, or [budding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Budding "Budding"); [mitosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitosis "Mitosis") and [meiosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meiosis "Meiosis") do not occur, so if a species of archaea exists in more than one form, all have the same genetic material.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Bergey-110)[Cell division](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_division "Cell division") is controlled in a [cell cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_cycle "Cell cycle"); after the cell's [chromosome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chromosome "Chromosome") is replicated and the two daughter chromosomes separate, the cell divides.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Bernander-185) In the genus _[Sulfolobus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobus "Sulfolobus")_, the cycle has characteristics that are similar to both bacterial and eukaryotic systems. The chromosomes replicate from multiple starting points ([origins of replication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_replication "Origin of replication")) using [DNA polymerases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_polymerase "DNA polymerase") that resemble the equivalent eukaryotic enzymes.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-186)

In Methanobacteriati the cell division protein [FtsZ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FtsZ "FtsZ"), which forms a contracting ring around the cell, and the components of the [septum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Septum "Septum") that is constructed across the center of the cell, are similar to their bacterial equivalents.[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Bernander-185) In cren-[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-187)[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-188) and thaumarchaea,[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-189) the cell division machinery Cdv fulfills a similar role. This machinery is related to the eukaryotic ESCRT-III machinery which, while best known for its role in cell sorting, also has been seen to fulfill a role in separation between divided cell, suggesting an ancestral role in cell division.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid29551994-190)

Both bacteria and eukaryotes, but not archaea, make [spores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spore "Spore").[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-191) Some species of [Haloarchaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloarchaea "Haloarchaea") undergo [phenotypic switching](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenotypic_switching "Phenotypic switching") and grow as several different cell types, including thick-walled structures that are resistant to [osmotic shock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osmotic_shock "Osmotic shock") and allow the archaea to survive in water at low salt concentrations, but these are not reproductive structures and may instead help them reach new habitats.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-192)

[Quorum sensing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quorum_sensing "Quorum sensing") was originally thought to not exist in Archaea, but recent studies have shown evidence of some species being able to perform cross-talk through quorum sensing. Other studies have shown syntrophic interactions between archaea and bacteria during biofilm growth. Although research is limited in archaeal quorum sensing, some studies have uncovered LuxR proteins in archaeal species, displaying similarities with bacteria LuxR, and ultimately allowing for the detection of small molecules that are used in high density communication. Similarly to bacteria, Archaea LuxR solos have shown to bind to AHLs (lactones) and non-AHLs ligans, which is a large part in performing intraspecies, interspecies, and interkingdom communication through quorum sensing.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-193)

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg/250px-Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Morning-Glory_Hotspring.jpg)

Archaea that grow in the hot water of the Morning Glory [Hot Spring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hot_Spring "Hot Spring") in [Yellowstone National Park](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park "Yellowstone National Park") produce a bright colour

Archaea exist in a broad range of [habitats](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitat "Habitat"), are recognized as a major part of global [ecosystems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecosystem "Ecosystem"),[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-DeLong-20) and may represent about 20% of microbial cells in the oceans.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-194) However, the first-discovered archaeans were [extremophiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extremophile "Extremophile").[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-valentine-140) Indeed, some archaea survive high temperatures, often above 100°C (212°F), as found in [geysers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geyser "Geyser"), [black smokers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_smoker "Black smoker"), and oil wells. Other common habitats include very cold habitats and highly [saline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt "Salt"), [acidic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid "Acid"), or [alkaline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alkaline "Alkaline") water, but archaea include [mesophiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesophile "Mesophile") that grow in mild conditions, in [swamps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swamp "Swamp") and [marshland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marsh "Marsh"), [sewage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sewage "Sewage"), the [oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ocean "Ocean"), the [intestinal tract](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intestinal_tract "Intestinal tract") of animals, and [soils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soil "Soil").[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Chow_2022-8)[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-DeLong-20) Similar to [PGPR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plant_growth-promoting_rhizobacteria "Plant growth-promoting rhizobacteria"), Archaea are considered a source of plant growth promotion as well.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Chow_2022-8)

Extremophile archaea are members of four main [physiological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physiological "Physiological") groups. These are the [halophiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halophile "Halophile"), [thermophiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermophile "Thermophile"), [alkaliphiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alkaliphile "Alkaliphile"), and [acidophiles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acidophile_(organisms) "Acidophile (organisms)").[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Pikuta-195) These groups are not comprehensive or phylum-specific, nor are they mutually exclusive, since some archaea belong to several groups. Nonetheless, they are a useful starting point for classification.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid28777382-196)

Halophiles, including the genus _[Halobacterium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Halobacterium "Halobacterium")_, live in extremely saline environments such as [salt lakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salt_lake "Salt lake") and outnumber their bacterial counterparts at salinities greater than 20–25%.[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-valentine-140) Thermophiles grow best at temperatures above 45°C (113°F), in places such as hot springs; _hyperthermophilic_ archaea grow optimally at temperatures greater than 80°C (176°F).[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-197) The archaeal _[Methanopyrus kandleri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanopyrus\_kandleri "Methanopyrus kandleri")_ Strain 116 can even reproduce at 122°C (252°F), the highest recorded temperature of any organism.[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-198)

Other archaea exist in very acidic or alkaline conditions.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Pikuta-195) For example, one of the most extreme archaean acidophiles is _[Picrophilus torridus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Picrophilus "Picrophilus")_, which grows at pH 0, which is equivalent to thriving in 1.2[molar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molar_concentration "Molar concentration")[sulfuric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfuric_acid "Sulfuric acid").[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-199)

This resistance to extreme environments has made archaea the focus of speculation about the possible properties of [extraterrestrial life](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life "Extraterrestrial life").[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-200) Some extremophile habitats are not dissimilar to those on [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars"),[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-201) leading to the suggestion that viable microbes could be transferred between planets in [meteorites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorite "Meteorite").[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-202)

Recently, several studies have shown that archaea exist not only in mesophilic and thermophilic environments but are also present, sometimes in high numbers, at low temperatures as well. For example, archaea are common in cold oceanic environments such as polar seas.[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-203) Even more significant are the large numbers of archaea found throughout the world's oceans in non-extreme habitats among the [plankton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plankton "Plankton") community (as part of the [picoplankton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Picoplankton "Picoplankton")).[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Karner-204) Although these archaea can be present in extremely high numbers (up to 40% of the microbial biomass), almost none of these species have been isolated and studied in [pure culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pure_culture "Pure culture").[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-205) Consequently, our understanding of the role of archaea in ocean ecology is rudimentary, so their full influence on global [biogeochemical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogeochemistry "Biogeochemistry") cycles remains largely unexplored.[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-206) Some marine Thermoproteota are capable of [nitrification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrification "Nitrification"), suggesting these organisms may affect the oceanic [nitrogen cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle "Nitrogen cycle"),[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceD-152) although these oceanic Thermoproteota may also use other sources of energy.[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-207)

Vast numbers of archaea are also found in the [sediments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sediment "Sediment") that cover the [sea floor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_floor "Sea floor"), with these organisms making up the majority of living cells at depths over 1 meter below the ocean bottom.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-208)[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-209) It has been demonstrated that in all oceanic surface sediments (from 1,000- to 10,000-m water depth), the impact of viral infection is higher on archaea than on bacteria and virus-induced lysis of archaea accounts for up to one-third of the total microbial biomass killed, resulting in the release of ~0.3 to 0.5 gigatons of carbon per year globally.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-210)

### Role in chemical cycling

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Role in chemical cycling")]

Archaea recycle elements such as [carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon"), [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen"), and [sulfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur") through their various habitats.[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pmid29753224-211) Archaea carry out many steps in the [nitrogen cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle "Nitrogen cycle"). This includes both reactions that remove nitrogen from ecosystems (such as [nitrate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrate "Nitrate")-based respiration and [denitrification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denitrification "Denitrification")) as well as processes that introduce nitrogen (such as nitrate assimilation and [nitrogen fixation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation "Nitrogen fixation")).[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-212)[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-213)

Researchers recently discovered archaeal involvement in [ammonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammonia "Ammonia") oxidation reactions. These reactions are particularly important in the oceans.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-ReferenceB-153)[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-214) The archaea also appear crucial for ammonia oxidation in soils. They produce [nitrite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrite "Nitrite"), which other microbes then oxidize to [nitrate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrate "Nitrate"). Plants and other organisms consume the latter.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-LeiningerUrich2006-215)

In the [sulfur cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur_cycle "Sulfur cycle"), archaea that grow by oxidizing [sulfur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfur "Sulfur") compounds release this element from rocks, making it available to other organisms, but the archaea that do this, such as _Sulfolobus_, produce [sulfuric acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfuric_acid "Sulfuric acid") as a waste product, and the growth of these organisms in abandoned mines can contribute to [acid mine drainage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acid_mine_drainage "Acid mine drainage") and other environmental damage.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Baker2003-216)

In the [carbon cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_cycle "Carbon cycle"), methanogen archaea remove hydrogen and play an important role in the decay of organic matter by the populations of microorganisms that act as [decomposers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decomposer "Decomposer") in anaerobic ecosystems, such as sediments, marshes, and [sewage-treatment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sewage_treatment "Sewage treatment") works.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-217)

### Interactions with other organisms

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Interactions with other organisms")]

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg/250px-Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Coptotermes_formosanus_shiraki_USGov_k8204-7.jpg)

Methanogenic archaea form a [symbiosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbiosis "Symbiosis") with [termites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Termite "Termite"), living in their gut and helping to digest cellulose.

The well-characterized interactions between archaea and other organisms are either [mutual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutualism_(biology) "Mutualism (biology)") or [commensal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commensalism "Commensalism"). There are no clear examples of known archaeal [pathogens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathogen "Pathogen") or [parasites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parasite "Parasite"),[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-218)[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-219) but some species of methanogens have been suggested to be involved in [infections in the mouth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Periodontal_disease "Periodontal disease"),[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-220)[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-221) and _[Nanoarchaeum equitans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoarchaeum\_equitans "Nanoarchaeum equitans")_ may be a parasite of another species of archaea, since it only survives and reproduces within the cells of the Crenarchaeon _[Ignicoccus hospitalis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ignicoccus "Ignicoccus")_,[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-pnas.org-158) and appears to offer no benefit to its [host](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Host_(biology) "Host (biology)").[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-222)

Mutualism is an interaction between individuals of different species that results in positive (beneficial) effects on per capita reproduction and/or survival of the interacting populations. One well-understood example of [mutualism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutualism_(biology) "Mutualism (biology)") is the interaction between [protozoa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protozoa "Protozoa") and [methanogenic archaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen") in the digestive tracts of animals that digest [cellulose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cellulose "Cellulose"), such as [ruminants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ruminant "Ruminant") and [termites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Termite "Termite").[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Chaban-223) In these anaerobic environments, protozoa break down plant cellulose to obtain energy. This process releases hydrogen as a waste product, but high levels of hydrogen reduce energy production. When methanogens convert hydrogen to methane, protozoa benefit from more energy.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-224)

In anaerobic protozoa, such as _Plagiopyla frontata_, _Trimyema_, _Heterometopus_ and _Metopus contortus_, archaea reside inside the protozoa and consume hydrogen produced in their [hydrogenosomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogenosome "Hydrogenosome").[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-225)[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-226)[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-227)[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Lange2005-228)[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-229) Archaea associate with larger organisms, too. For example, the marine archaean _[Cenarchaeum symbiosum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cenarchaeum "Cenarchaeum")_ is an [endosymbiont](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endosymbiont "Endosymbiont") of the [sponge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sponge "Sponge")_[Axinella mexicana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axinella "Axinella")_.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Preston1996-230)

Some archaea are commensals, benefiting from an association without helping or harming the other organism. For example, the methanogen _[Methanobrevibacter smithii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanobrevibacter\_smithii "Methanobrevibacter smithii")_ is by far the most common archaean in the [human flora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_flora "Human flora"), making up about one in ten of the prokaryotes in the human gut.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-231) In termites and in humans, these methanogens may in fact be mutualists, interacting with other microbes in the gut to aid digestion.[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-232) Archaean communities associate with a range of other organisms, such as on the surface of [corals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coral "Coral"),[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-233) and in the region of soil that surrounds plant roots (the [rhizosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhizosphere_(ecology) "Rhizosphere (ecology)")).[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-234)[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-235)

Although Archaea do not have a historical reputation of being pathogens, Archaea are often found with similar genomes to more common pathogens like _E. coli,_[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-236) showing metabolic links and evolutionary history with today's pathogens. Archaea have been inconsistently detected in clinical studies because of the lack of categorization of Archaea into more specific species.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-237)

Significance in technology and industry
---------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Archaea&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Significance in technology and industry")]

[Extremophile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extremophile "Extremophile") archaea, particularly those resistant either to heat or to extremes of acidity and alkalinity, are a source of [enzymes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enzyme "Enzyme") that function under these harsh conditions.[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-238)[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Egorova-239) These enzymes have found many uses. For example, thermostable [DNA polymerases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_polymerase "DNA polymerase"), such as the [Pfu DNA polymerase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pfu_DNA_polymerase "Pfu DNA polymerase") from _[Pyrococcus furiosus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrococcus\_furiosus "Pyrococcus furiosus")_, revolutionized [molecular biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_biology "Molecular biology") by allowing the [polymerase chain reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction "Polymerase chain reaction") to be used in research as a simple and rapid technique for [cloning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cloning "Cloning") DNA. In industry, [amylases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amylase "Amylase"), [galactosidases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galactosidases "Galactosidases") and [pullulanases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pullulanase "Pullulanase") in other species of _[Pyrococcus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrococcus "Pyrococcus")_ that function at over 100°C (212°F) allow [food processing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_processing "Food processing") at high temperatures, such as the production of low [lactose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lactose "Lactose") milk and [whey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whey "Whey").[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-240) Enzymes from these thermophilic archaea also tend to be very stable in organic solvents, allowing their use in environmentally friendly processes in [green chemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green_chemistry "Green chemistry") that synthesize organic compounds.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-Egorova-239) This stability makes them easier to use in [structural biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structural_biology "Structural biology"). Consequently, the counterparts of bacterial or eukaryotic enzymes from extremophile archaea are often used in structural studies.[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-241)

In contrast with the range of applications of archaean enzymes, the use of the organisms themselves in biotechnology is less developed. [Methanogenic archaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Methanogen "Methanogen") are a vital part of [sewage treatment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sewage_treatment "Sewage treatment"), since they are part of the community of microorganisms that carry out [anaerobic digestion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion "Anaerobic digestion") and produce [biogas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biogas "Biogas").[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-242) In [mineral processing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mineral_processing "Mineral processing"), acidophilic archaea display promise for the extraction of metals from [ores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ore "Ore"), including [gold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gold "Gold"), [cobalt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cobalt "Cobalt") and [copper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copper "Copper").[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-243)

Archaea host a new class of potentially useful [antibiotics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibiotic "Antibiotic"). A few of these [archaeocins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaeocin "Archaeocin") have been characterized, but hundreds more are believed to exist, especially within _[Haloarchaea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haloarchaea "Haloarchaea")_ and _[Sulfolobus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulfolobus "Sulfolobus")_. These compounds differ in structure from bacterial antibiotics, so they may have novel modes of action. In addition, they may allow the creation of new [selectable markers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selectable_marker "Selectable marker") for use in archaeal molecular biology.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaea#cite_note-244)
